        "question": "How can you graphically represent the relationship of ratios among two quantities?",
        "solution": "Solution:\n\nAnswer approach — use a visual model that preserves the relative sizes (proportions) so you can read or compute corresponding values. Common graphical methods are a ratio chart (double-number-line or tape diagram) and a scale drawing (bar model or scaled segments). These show equivalent ratios, unit ratio, and allow finding unknowns by scaling.\n\nRatio chart / double-number-line (steps)\n1. Put the two quantities on parallel lines (or two stacked bars). Mark equal “units” along each line so corresponding marks represent the same ratio unit.  \n2. Label one unit as the pair from the given ratio. For ratio [math]3:5[/math], one unit corresponds to [math]3[/math] on the first line and [math]5[/math] on the second.  \n3. To find equivalent pairs, multiply the unit by the same integer (scale factor). For example, to get four units: first = [math]3×4=12[/math], second = [math]5×4=20[/math].  \n4. If one quantity is known, divide by its unit amount to get the scale factor, then multiply the other unit by that factor to get the unknown.\n\nWorked example (ratio [math]3:5[/math], given first quantity [math]12[/math], find second):\n- Unit size for first = [math]3[/math]. Scale factor = [math]12 ÷ 3 = 4[/math].  \n- Second = [math]5 × 4 = 20[/math].  \nGraphically you would draw two parallel lines, mark four unit segments on each; the top line totals [math]12[/math], the bottom totals [math]20[/math].\n\nScale drawing / bar model (steps)\n1. Choose a convenient length to represent one unit of the ratio (e.g., 1 unit = 1 cm).  \n2. Draw adjacent bars or segments in the ratio lengths (for [math]3:5[/math], draw one bar of length [math]3u[/math] and another of length [math]5u[/math]).  \n3. If actual values are given, find the unit length by dividing the known value by its bar-length in units, then scale the other bar accordingly to get the corresponding value.  \nExample: if the bar of length [math]3u[/math] must represent [math]12[/math] cm, then [math]u = 12 ÷ 3 = 4[/math] cm, so the other bar is [math]5u = 5×4 = 20[/math] cm.\n\nOther visual options (when useful)\n- Pie chart: good to show parts of a whole (proportional shares).  \n- Stacked bar or segmented tape diagram: useful for multi-part ratios or comparing several ratios.\n\nKey concepts to keep in mind\n- Equivalent ratios are produced by multiplying both parts by the same factor.  \n- Unit ratio (one unit) simplifies finding scale factors.  \n- Graphical methods preserve proportional relationships visually, making reasoning and computation easier.\n\nThis approach gives a clear visual representation and a straightforward calculation method to move between units and find unknown quantities.",

        "question": "What does it mean for a figure to have rotational symmetry, and can you give an example?",
        "solution": "Solution:\nA figure has rotational symmetry if there exists a point (the center) and an angle of rotation, less than [math]360^\\circ[/math], such that when the figure is rotated about that center by that angle the figure coincides exactly with its original position.\n\nApproach and key ideas:\n- Rotational symmetry means invariance under a rotation about a center point.\n- The smallest positive angle that maps the figure onto itself is called the angle of rotation; the number of times that rotation fits into [math]360^\\circ[/math] is the order of rotational symmetry.\n\nWorking steps and example:\n1. State the condition: a rotation by some [math]\\theta\u003C/math] (with [math]0^\\circ\u003C\\theta\u003C360^\\circ[/math]) about the center leaves the figure unchanged.\n2. Example — a square:\n   - Rotate the square about its center by [math]90^\\circ[/math]. The square's vertices and edges move to positions occupied by other vertices and edges, so the square looks identical after the rotation.\n   - The smallest such angle is [math]90^\\circ[/math], so the order of rotational symmetry is [math]360^\\circ/90^\\circ=4[/math].\n3. Other examples for context: a regular equilateral triangle has rotational symmetry with smallest angle [math]120^\\circ[/math] (order 3); a circle has infinite rotational symmetry because any rotation maps it to itself.\n\nLogical reasoning summary:\nIf a rotation by some nonzero angle brings the figure back onto itself, then every multiple of that angle (up to [math]360^\\circ[/math]) will too; the count of distinct such rotations is the order of symmetry. The square demonstrates this clearly with four equal rotations of [math]90^\\circ[/math].",
        "chapter_id": "2546",

For example, a square rotated about its center by 90°, 180°, or 270° coincides with its original position, so it has rotational symmetry.",
